         </a>
        </sup>

     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Empire State Building
      </b>
      is a 102-story
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyscrapercenter_8-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-skyscrapercenter-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emporis_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-emporis-9">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skypage_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-skypage-10">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_count_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Floor_count-11">
        [note 2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Early_skyscrapers" title="Early skyscrapers">
       skyscraper
      </a>
      located in
      <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
       Midtown Manhattan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
       New York City
      </a>
      , on
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
       Fifth Avenue
      </a>
      between West
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/33rd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="33rd Street (Manhattan)">
       33rd
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/34th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="34th Street (Manhattan)">
       34th
      </a>
      Streets. It has a roof height of 1,250 feet (381 m), and with its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_tower" title="Antenna tower">
       antenna
      </a>
      spire included, it stands a total of 1,454 feet (443 m) high.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyscrapercenter_8-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-skyscrapercenter-8">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its name is derived from the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_nicknames" title="List of U.S. state nicknames">
       nickname
      </a>
      for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">
       New York
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_State" title="Empire State">
       Empire State
      </a>
      . It stood as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings and structures in the world">
       world's tallest building
      </a>
      for nearly 40 years, from its completion in early 1931 until the
      <a href="/wiki/Topping_out" title="Topping out">
       topping out
      </a>
      of the original
      <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">
       World Trade Center
      </a>
      's North Tower in late 1970.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the
      <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
       September 11 attacks
      </a>
      in 2001, the Empire State Building was again the tallest building in New York, until
      <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">
       One World Trade Center
      </a>
      reached a greater height in April 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Empire State Building is currently the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_United_States" title="List of tallest buildings in the United States">
       fifth-tallest completed skyscraper in the United States
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">
       29th-tallest in the world
      </a>
      . It is also the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">
       fifth-tallest freestanding structure in the Americas
      </a>
      . When measured by
      <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle" title="Pinnacle">
       pinnacle
      </a>
      height, it is the fourth-tallest building in the United States.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Empire State Building is an
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       American
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">
       cultural icon
      </a>
      . It is designed in the distinctive
      <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">
       Art Deco
      </a>
      style and has been named as one of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Modern_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Modern World">
       Seven Wonders of the Modern World
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Civil_Engineers" title="American Society of Civil Engineers">
       American Society of Civil Engineers
      </a>
      . The building and its street floor interior are designated landmarks of the
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Landmarks_Preservation_Commission" title="New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission">
       New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission
      </a>
      , and confirmed by the
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Board_of_Estimate" title="New York City Board of Estimate">
       New York City Board of Estimate
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AIA-18">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was designated as a
      <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">
       National Historic Landmark
      </a>
      in 1986.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlsum_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nhlsum-14">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlnom_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nhlnom-19">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrhpphotos_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nrhpphotos-20">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2007, it was ranked number one on the AIA's List of
      <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Favorite_Architecture" title="America's Favorite Architecture">
       America's Favorite Architecture
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The building is owned by the Empire State Realty Trust, of which Anthony Malkin serves as Chairman, CEO and President.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2010, the Empire State Building underwent a $550 million renovation, with $120 million spent to transform the building into a more
      <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">
       energy efficient
      </a>
      and eco-friendly structure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-22">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Empire State Building is the tallest
      <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">
       Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED)
      </a>
      -certified building in the United States, having received a gold LEED rating in September 2011.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inhabitat1_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inhabitat1-23">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The site of the Empire State Building was first developed as the John Thompson Farm in the late 18th century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the time, a stream ran across the site, emptying into Sunfish Pond, located a block away. Beginning in the late 19th century, the block was occupied by the
      <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">
       Waldorf-Astoria Hotel
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      frequented by
      <a href="/wiki/Ward_McAllister#.22The_Four_Hundred.22" title="Ward McAllister">
       The Four Hundred
      </a>
      , the social elite of New York.
     </p>
     <p>
      The limestone for the Empire State Building came from the Empire Mill in Sanders, Indiana which is an unincorporated town adjacent to Bloomington, Indiana. The Empire Mill Land office is near State Road 37 and Old State Road 37 just south of Bloomington. The Bloomington, Bedford, and Oolitic area is known locally as the limestone capital of the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetersonNYT1981_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PetersonNYT1981-26">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoadsideAmerica2016_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RoadsideAmerica2016-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_construction">
       Design and construction
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="3000" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/250px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/375px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/500px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A worker bolts beams during construction; the
        <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">
         Chrysler Building
        </a>
        can be seen in the background.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Empire State Building was designed by
      <a href="/wiki/William_F._Lamb" title="William F. Lamb">
       William F. Lamb
      </a>
      from the architectural firm
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shreve,_Lamb_and_Harmon" title="Shreve, Lamb and Harmon">
       Shreve, Lamb and Harmon
      </a>
      , which produced the building drawings in just two weeks, using its earlier designs for the
      <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_Building" title="Reynolds Building">
       Reynolds Building
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Winston-Salem,_North_Carolina" title="Winston-Salem, North Carolina">
       Winston-Salem
      </a>
      , North Carolina, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Carew_Tower" title="Carew Tower">
       Carew Tower
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">
       Cincinnati
      </a>
      , Ohio (designed by the architectural firm
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_W._Ahlschlager" title="Walter W. Ahlschlager">
       W. W. Ahlschlager &amp; Associates
      </a>
      ) as a basis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Every year the staff of the Empire State Building sends a Father's Day card to the staff at the Reynolds Building in Winston-Salem to pay homage to its role as predecessor to the Empire State Building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Covington2012_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Covington2012-30">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The building was designed from the top down.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The general contractors were The Starrett Brothers and Eken, and the project was financed primarily by
      <a href="/wiki/John_J._Raskob" title="John J. Raskob">
       John J. Raskob
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pierre_S._du_Pont" title="Pierre S. du Pont">
       Pierre S. du Pont
      </a>
      . The construction company was chaired by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith" title="Alfred E. Smith">
       Alfred E. Smith
      </a>
      , a former
      <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">
       Governor of New York
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">
       James Farley
      </a>
      's General Builders Supply Corporation supplied the building materials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc-NY-ESB_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Enc-NY-ESB-2">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/John_W._Bowser" title="John W. Bowser">
       John W. Bowser
      </a>
      was project construction superintendent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Excavation of the site began on January 22, 1930, and construction on the building itself started on March 17—St. Patrick's Day—per Al Smith's influence as Empire State, Inc. president. The project involved 3,400 workers, mostly immigrants from Europe, along with hundreds of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohawk_nation" title="Mohawk nation">
       Mohawk
      </a>
      iron workers, many from the
      <a href="/wiki/Kahnawake" title="Kahnawake">
       Kahnawake
      </a>
      reserve near
      <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">
       Montreal
      </a>
      . According to official accounts, five workers died during the construction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Governor Smith's grandchildren cut the ribbon on May 1, 1931. Lewis Wickes Hine's photography of the construction provides not only invaluable documentation of the construction, but also a glimpse into common day life of workers in that era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The construction was part of an intense competition in New York for the title of "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">
       world's tallest building
      </a>
      ". Two other projects fighting for the title,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">
       40 Wall Street
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">
       Chrysler Building
      </a>
      , were still under construction when work began on the Empire State Building. Each held the title for less than a year, as the Empire State Building surpassed them upon its completion, on April 11, 1931, 12 days ahead of schedule, just 410 days after construction commenced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gencon_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gencon-3">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The building was officially opened on May 1, 1931 in dramatic fashion, when United States President
      <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">
       Herbert Hoover
      </a>
      turned on the building's lights with the push of a button from Washington, D.C. Ironically, the first use of tower lights atop the Empire State Building, the following year, was for the purpose of signaling the victory of
      <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">
       Franklin D. Roosevelt
      </a>
      over Hoover in the presidential election of November 1932.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Opening">
       Opening
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9000" data-file-width="3865" height="408" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/175px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/263px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/350px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg 2x" width="175"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        View of the building from the north
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The building's opening coincided with the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">
       Great Depression
      </a>
      in the United States, and as a result much of its office space was initially unrented. The building's vacancy was exacerbated by its poor location on 34th Street, which placed it relatively far from public transportation, as
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">
       Grand Central Terminal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">
       Penn Station
      </a>
      , built decades beforehand, are several blocks away, as is the more recently built
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Authority_Bus_Terminal" title="Port Authority Bus Terminal">
       Port Authority Bus Terminal
      </a>
      . Other more successful skyscrapers, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">
       Chrysler Building
      </a>
      , did not have this problem. In its first year of operation, the observation deck took in approximately 2 million dollars, as much money as its owners made in rent that year. The lack of renters led New Yorkers to deride the building as the "Empty State Building".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The building only became profitable in 1950. The 1951 sale of the Empire State Building to
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_L._Stevens" title="Roger L. Stevens">
       Roger L. Stevens
      </a>
      and his business partners was brokered by the prominent upper Manhattan real-estate firm Charles F. Noyes &amp; Company for a record $51 million. At the time, that was the highest price paid for a single structure in real-estate history.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Incidents">
       Incidents
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="2640" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/250px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/375px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/500px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A night view from the observatory, looking south
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1945_plane_crash">
       1945 plane crash
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/B-25_Empire_State_Building_crash" title="B-25 Empire State Building crash">
       B-25 Empire State Building crash
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      At 9:40 am on Saturday, July 28, 1945, a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-25_Mitchell" title="B-25 Mitchell">
       B-25 Mitchell
      </a>
      bomber, piloted in thick fog by
      <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_colonel_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant colonel (United States)">
       Lieutenant Colonel
      </a>
      William Franklin Smith, Jr.,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      crashed into the north side of the Empire State Building, between the 79th and 80th floors, where the offices of the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Catholic_Welfare_Council" title="National Catholic Welfare Council">
       National Catholic Welfare Council
      </a>
      were located. One engine shot through the side opposite the impact and flew as far as the next block, where it landed on the roof of a nearby building, starting a fire that destroyed a penthouse. The other engine and part of the landing gear plummeted down an elevator shaft. The resulting fire was extinguished in 40 minutes. Fourteen people were killed in the incident.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Elevator operator Betty Lou Oliver survived a plunge of 75 stories inside an elevator, which still stands as the
      <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">
       Guinness World Record
      </a>
      for the longest survived elevator fall recorded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite the damage and loss of life, the building was open for business on many floors on the following Monday. The crash helped spur the passage of the long-pending
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_Tort_Claims_Act" title="Federal Tort Claims Act">
       Federal Tort Claims Act
      </a>
      of 1946, as well as the insertion of retroactive provisions into the law, allowing people to sue the government for the incident.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A year later, another aircraft narrowly missed striking the building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-height_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-height-46">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide_attempts">
       Suicide attempts
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Over the years, more than 30 people have
      <a href="/wiki/Jumper_(suicide)" title="Jumper (suicide)">
       attempted suicide
      </a>
      , most successfully, by jumping from the upper parts of the building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suicide1_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-suicide1-47">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first suicide occurred even before its completion, by a worker who had been laid off. The fence around the observatory terrace was put up in 1947 after five people tried to jump during a three-week span.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suicide2_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-suicide2-48">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On December 16, 1943, ex-
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
       United States Navy
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Gunner%27s_mate" title="Gunner's mate">
       gunner's mate
      </a>
      William Lloyd Rambo, 22, jumped to his death from the 86th floor, landing amidst Christmas shoppers on the street below.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the early morning of September 27, 1946,
      <a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">
       shell-shocked
      </a>
      27-year-old Marine Douglas W. Brashear, Jr. jumped from the 76th-floor window of the Grant Advertising Agency after phoning a co-worker to tell her "I know now this is the end." Police found his shoes 50 feet from his body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On May 1, 1947, 23-year-old
      <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_McHale" title="Evelyn McHale">
       Evelyn McHale
      </a>
      leapt to her death from the 86th floor observation deck and landed on a
      <a href="/wiki/Limousine" title="Limousine">
       limousine
      </a>
      parked at the curb. Photography student Robert Wiles took a photo of McHale's oddly intact corpse a few minutes after her death. The police found a suicide note among possessions she left on the observation deck: "He is much better off without me ... I wouldn’t make a good wife for anybody". The photo ran in the edition of May 12, 1947 of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">
        Life
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is often referred to as "The Most Beautiful Suicide". It was later used by visual artist
      <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">
       Andy Warhol
      </a>
      in one of his prints entitled
      <i>
       Suicide (Fallen Body)
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Only one person has jumped from the upper observatory: on November 3, 1932, Frederick Eckert, of
      <a href="/wiki/Astoria,_Queens" title="Astoria, Queens">
       Astoria
      </a>
      , ran past a guard in the enclosed 102nd floor gallery and jumped a gate leading to an outdoor catwalk intended for
      <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">
       dirigible
      </a>
      passengers. He landed and died on the roof of the 86th floor observation promenade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Two people have survived jumps, in both cases by not falling more than a floor: On December 2, 1979, Elvita Adams jumped from the 86th floor, only to be blown back onto a ledge on the 85th floor by a gust of wind and left with a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broken_hip" title="Broken hip">
       broken hip
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On April 25, 2013, a man, who is presumed to have jumped, fell from the 86th floor observation deck but landed alive on an 85th floor ledge – where security guards brought him inside with minor injuries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Shootings">
       Shootings
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Two major shooting incidents have occurred at or in front of the Empire State Building.
     </p>
     <p>
      On February 23, 1997, at about 5 p.m.
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">
       EST
      </a>
      , a gunman
      <a href="/wiki/1997_Empire_State_Building_shooting" title="1997 Empire State Building shooting">
       shot seven people
      </a>
      on the 86th floor observation deck. Abu Kamal, a 69-year-old Palestinian teacher, killed one person and wounded six others, supposedly in response to events happening in
      <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">
       Palestine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      , before committing suicide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On August 24, 2012 at about 9 a.m. EDT, on the sidewalk at the
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
       Fifth Avenue
      </a>
      side of the building, a gunman
      <a href="/wiki/2012_Empire_State_Building_shooting" title="2012 Empire State Building shooting">
       shot and killed a former co-worker
      </a>
      from a workplace that had laid him off in 2011. When two police officers confronted the gunman, 58-year-old Jeffrey T. Johnson, he aimed his firearm at them. They responded by firing 16 shots at Johnson, killing him but also wounding nine bystanders, most of whom were hit by fragments, although three took direct hits from bullets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CNN-59">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">
       Architecture
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="426" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg/220px-Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg/330px-Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Empire State Building as seen across the
        <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
         East River
        </a>
        , from
        <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">
         Brooklyn
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/220px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/330px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/440px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A series of
        <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">
         setbacks
        </a>
        causes the building to taper with height.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">
       Interior
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Empire State Building rises to 1,250 ft (381 m) at the 102nd floor, and including the 203 ft (62 m) pinnacle, its full height reaches 1,453 feet
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       8
       <span class="visualhide">
       </span>
       <sup>
        9
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        16
       </sub>
      </span>
      inches (443.092 m). The building has 85 stories of commercial and office space representing 2,158,000 sq ft (200,500 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) of rentable space. It has an indoor and outdoor observation deck on the 86th floor. The remaining 16 stories represent the
      <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">
       Art Deco
      </a>
      tower, which is capped by a 102nd-floor observatory. Atop the tower is the 203 ft (62 m) pinnacle, much of which is covered by broadcast antennas, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">
       lightning rod
      </a>
      at the very top.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Empire State Building was the first building to have more than 100 floors. It has 6,500 windows and 73 elevators, and there are 1,860 steps from street level to the 102nd floor. It has a total floor area of 2,768,591 sq ft (257,211 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ); the base of the Empire State Building is about 2 acres (8,094 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ). The building houses 1,000 businesses and has its own ZIP code, 10118. As of 2007, approximately 21,000 employees work in the building each day, making the Empire State Building the second-largest single office complex in America, after
      <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">
       the Pentagon
      </a>
      . The building was completed in one year and 45 days. Its original 64 elevators are located in a central core;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      today, the Empire State Building has 73 elevators in all, including service elevators. It takes less than one minute by elevator to get to the 80th floor, which contains a gift shop and an exhibit detailing the building's construction. From there, visitors can take another elevator or climb the stairs to the 86th floor, where an outdoor observation deck is located. The building has 70 mi (113 km) of pipe, 2,500,000 ft (760,000 m) of electrical wire,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and about 9,000 faucets.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      It is heated by low-pressure steam; despite its height, the building only requires between 2 and 3 psi (14 and 21 kPa) of steam pressure for heating. It weighs approximately 370,000 short tons (340,000 t). The exterior of the building is clad in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indiana_limestone" title="Indiana limestone">
       Indiana limestone
      </a>
      panels.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Empire State Building cost $40,948,900 to build
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cost_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cost-5">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (equivalent to $638,247,300 in 2016). Long-term forecasting of the life cycle of the structure was implemented at the design phase to ensure that the building's future intended uses were not restricted by the requirements of previous generations. This is particularly evident in the over-design of the building's electrical system.
     </p>
     <p>
      The building's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_deco" title="Art deco">
       art deco
      </a>
      design is typical of pre–World War II architecture in New York. The modernistic
      <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">
       stainless steel
      </a>
      canopies of the entrances on 33rd and 34th Streets lead to two story-high corridors around the elevator core, crossed by stainless steel and glass-enclosed bridges at the second-floor level. The elevator core contains 67 elevators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AIA-18">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The lobby is three stories high and features an aluminum relief of the skyscraper without the antenna, which was not added to the spire until 1952. The north corridor contained eight illuminated panels, created by Roy Sparkia and Renée Nemorov in 1963 in time for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1964_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 World's Fair">
       1964 World's Fair
      </a>
      , which depicts the building as the
      <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Wonder_of_the_World" title="Eighth Wonder of the World">
       Eighth Wonder of the World
      </a>
      , alongside the traditional seven. These panels were eventually moved near a ticketing line for the observation deck.
     </p>
     <p>
      Until the 1960s, the ceilings in the lobby had a shiny art deco mural inspired by both the sky and the
      <a href="/wiki/Machine_Age" title="Machine Age">
       Machine Age
      </a>
      , until it was covered with ceiling tiles and fluorescent lighting. Because the original murals, designed by an artist named Leif Neandross, were damaged, reproductions were installed. Over 50 artists and workers used 15,000 square feet of aluminum and 1,300 square feet of 23-carat gold leaf to re-create the mural. Renovations to the lobby alluded to original plans for the building; replacing the clock over the information desk in the Fifth Avenue lobby with an
      <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">
       anemometer
      </a>
      , as well as installing two chandeliers originally intended to be part of the building when it first opened.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2000, the building's owners installed a series of paintings by the New York artist
      <a href="/wiki/Kysa_Johnson" title="Kysa Johnson">
       Kysa Johnson
      </a>
      in the concourse level. In January 2014 the artist filed suit in federal court in New York under the
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_Artists_Rights_Act" title="Visual Artists Rights Act">
       Visual Artists Rights Act
      </a>
      , alleging the negligent destruction of the paintings and damage to her reputation as an artist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The building's lobbies and common areas received a $550 million renovation in 2009, which included new air conditioning, waterproofing, and renovating the observation deck; moving the gift shop to the 80th floor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of this, $120 million was spent in an effort to transform the building into a more
      <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">
       energy efficient
      </a>
      and eco-friendly structure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-22">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, the 6,500 windows were remanufactured onsite into
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Superwindows&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Superwindows (page does not exist)">
       superwindows
      </a>
      which block heat but pass light.
      <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">
       Air conditioning
      </a>
      operating costs on hot days were reduced and this saved $17 million of the project's capital cost immediately, partly funding other retrofitting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Receiving a gold
      <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">
       Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED)
      </a>
      rating in September 2011, the Empire State Building is the tallest LEED certified building in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inhabitat1_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-inhabitat1-23">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/220px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/330px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/440px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Lighting at the top of the Empire State Building
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Features">
       Features
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Above_the_102nd_floor">
       <span id="Dirigible_.28airship.29_terminal">
       </span>
       Above the 102nd floor
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      On the 102nd floor of the Empire State Building there is a door with stairs ascending to the 103rd floor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWPFlr_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TWPFlr-66">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was built as a disembarkation floor for airships tethered to the building's spire, and has a circular balcony outside.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is now a hot spot for celebrities,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      and an access point to reach the spire for maintenance. The room now contains electrical equipment. Above the 103rd floor, there is a set of stairs and a ladder to reach the spire for maintenance work.
     </p>
     <p>
      The building's
      <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">
       Art Deco
      </a>
      spire was designed to be a mooring mast and depot for
      <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">
       dirigibles
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc-NY-Unbuilt_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Enc-NY-Unbuilt-68">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An elevator between the 86th and 102nd floors would carry passengers after they checked in on the 86th floor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The idea proved impractical and dangerous, due to the powerful updrafts caused by the building itself,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as the lack of mooring lines tying the other end of the craft to the ground.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The building's design was expanded to include the mooring mast as part of a competition for the world's tallest building.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A large broadcast tower was added atop the spire in the early 1950s, to support the transmission antennas of several television and FM stations. Until then, NBC had exclusive rights to the site, and – beginning in 1931 – built various, smaller antennas for their television transmissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enc-NY-Unbuilt_68-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Enc-NY-Unbuilt-68">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcast_stations">
       Broadcast stations
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      New York City is the largest media market in the United States. Since the September 11 attacks, nearly all of the city's commercial broadcast stations (both television and FM radio) have transmitted from the top of the Empire State Building, although a few FM stations are located at the nearby
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cond%C3%A9_Nast_Building" title="Condé Nast Building">
       Condé Nast Building
      </a>
      . Most New York City
      <a href="/wiki/AM_broadcasting" title="AM broadcasting">
       AM stations
      </a>
      broadcast from sites across the
      <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">
       Hudson River
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      or from other surrounding areas.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/220px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/330px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/440px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Antennae for broadcast stations are located at the top of the Empire State Building.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Broadcasting began at the Empire State Building on December 22, 1931, when
      <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">
       RCA
      </a>
      began transmitting experimental television broadcasts from a small antenna erected atop the spire. They leased the 85th floor and built a laboratory there, and—in 1934—RCA was joined by
      <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Howard_Armstrong" title="Edwin Howard Armstrong">
       Edwin Howard Armstrong
      </a>
      in a cooperative venture to test his FM system from the building's antenna. When Armstrong and RCA fell out in 1935 and his FM equipment was removed, the 85th floor became the home of RCA's New York television operations, first as experimental station W2XBS channel 1, which eventually became (on July 1, 1941) commercial station WNBT, channel 1 (now
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WNBC-TV" title="WNBC-TV">
       WNBC-TV
      </a>
      channel 4). NBC's FM station (WEAF-FM, now WQHT) began transmitting from the antenna in 1940. NBC retained exclusive use of the top of the building until 1950, when the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">
       FCC
      </a>
      ordered the exclusive deal broken, based on consumer complaints that a common location was necessary for the (now) seven New York-area television stations (five licensed to New York City, NY, one licensed to Newark, NJ, and one licensed to Secaucus, NJ) to transmit from so that receiving antennas would not have to be constantly adjusted. Construction on a giant tower began. Other television broadcasters then joined RCA at the building, on the 83rd, 82nd, and 81st floors, frequently bringing sister FM stations along for the ride. Multiple transmissions of TV and FM began from the new tower in 1951. In 1965, a separate set of FM antennas was constructed ringing the 103rd floor observation area to act as a master antenna. When the World Trade Center was being constructed, it caused serious reception problems for the television stations, most of which then moved to the World Trade Center as soon as it was completed. This made it possible to renovate the antenna structure and the transmitter facilities for the benefit of the FM stations remaining there, which were soon joined by other FMs and UHF TVs moving in from elsewhere in the metropolitan area. The destruction of the World Trade Center necessitated a great deal of shuffling of antennas and transmitter rooms to accommodate the stations moving back uptown.
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 2012, the Empire State Building is home to the following stations:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_broadcasting" title="Television broadcasting">
        Television broadcasting
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/WCBS-TV" title="WCBS-TV">
        WCBS-2
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WNBC-TV" title="WNBC-TV">
        WNBC-4
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WNYW" title="WNYW">
        WNYW-5
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WABC-TV" title="WABC-TV">
        WABC-7
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WWOR-TV" title="WWOR-TV">
        WWOR-9
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Secaucus,_New_Jersey" title="Secaucus, New Jersey">
        Secaucus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WPIX" title="WPIX">
        WPIX-11
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WNET" title="WNET">
        WNET-13
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">
        Newark
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WNYE-TV" title="WNYE-TV">
        WNYE-25
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WPXN-TV" title="WPXN-TV">
        WPXN-31
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WXTV-DT" title="WXTV-DT">

       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">
        Paterson
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WNJU" title="WNJU">
        WNJU-47
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Linden,_New_Jersey" title="Linden, New Jersey">
        Linden
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/WFUT-DT" title="WFUT-DT">
        WFUT-68
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">
        Newark
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">

       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/WBMP_(FM)" title="WBMP (FM)">

       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WPAT-FM" title="WPAT-FM">

       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">

       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/WNYC-FM" title="WNYC-FM">

       </a>

       <a href="/wiki/WPLJ" title="WPLJ">

       </a>

       <a href="/wiki/WXNY-FM" title="WXNY-FM">
